<p id="mwEA">The <b id="mwEQ">dog</b> (<i id="mwEg"><b id="mwEw">Canis familiaris</b></i><sup about="#mwt44" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Alvares2019_4-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Alvares2019"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Alvares2019-4" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 4;" id="mwFA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwFQ">[4]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt38" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWangTedford20081_5-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Wang"},"2":{"wt":"Tedford"},"3":{"wt":"2008"},"p":{"wt":"1"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWangTedford20081-5" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 5;" id="mwFg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwFw">[5]</span></a></sup> or <i id="mwGA"><b id="mwGQ">Canis lupus familiaris</b></i><sup about="#mwt41" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWangTedford20081_5-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Wang"},"2":{"wt":"Tedford"},"3":{"wt":"2008"},"p":{"wt":"1"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWangTedford20081-5" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 5;" id="mwGg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwGw">[5]</span></a></sup>) is a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Domesticated" title="Domesticated" class="mw-redirect" id="mwHA">domesticated</a> descendant of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wolf" title="Wolf" id="mwHQ">wolf</a>. Also called the <b id="mwHg">domestic dog</b>, it is <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Domestication_of_the_dog" title="Domestication of the dog" id="mwHw">derived</a> from the extinct <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pleistocene_wolf" title="Pleistocene wolf" id="mwIA">Pleistocene wolf</a>,<sup about="#mwt45" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Bergström2020_6-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Bergström2020"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Bergström2020-6" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 6;" id="mwIQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwIg">[6]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt46" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Frantz2020_7-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Frantz2020"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Frantz2020-7" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 7;" id="mwIw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwJA">[7]</span></a></sup> and the modern <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wolf" title="Wolf" id="mwJQ">wolf</a> is the dog's nearest living relative.<sup about="#mwt47" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-freedman2017_8-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"freedman2017"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-freedman2017-8" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 8;" id="mwJg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwJw">[8]</span></a></sup> Dogs were the first <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Species" title="Species" id="mwKA">species</a> to be domesticated<sup about="#mwt48" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-larson2014_9-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"larson2014"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-larson2014-9" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 9;" id="mwKQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwKg">[9]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt49" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-freedman2017_8-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"freedman2017"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-freedman2017-8" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 8;" id="mwKw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwLA">[8]</span></a></sup> by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer" id="mwLQ">hunter-gatherers</a> over 15,000 years ago<sup about="#mwt50" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Frantz2020_7-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Frantz2020"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Frantz2020-7" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 7;" id="mwLg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwLw">[7]</span></a></sup> before the development of agriculture.<sup about="#mwt51" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Thalmann2018_1-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Thalmann2018"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Thalmann2018-1" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 1;" id="mwMA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwMQ">[1]</span></a></sup> Due to their long association with humans, dogs have expanded to a large number of domestic individuals<sup about="#mwt52" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Ostrander2019_10-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Ostrander2019"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Ostrander2019-10" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 10;" id="mwMg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwMw">[10]</span></a></sup> and gained the ability to thrive on a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Starch" title="Starch" id="mwNA">starch</a>-rich diet that would be inadequate for other <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Canidae" title="Canidae" id="mwNQ">canids</a>.<sup about="#mwt56" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-axelssonetal2013_11-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"axelssonetal2013"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-axelssonetal2013-11"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-axelssonetal2013-11" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 11;" id="mwNg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwNw">[11]</span></a></sup></p> 

<p id="mwOA">The dog has been <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Selectively_bred" title="Selectively bred" class="mw-redirect" id="mwOQ">selectively bred</a> over millennia for various behaviors, sensory capabilities, and physical attributes.<sup about="#mwt60" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-ADW_12-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"ADW"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-ADW-12" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 12;" id="mwOg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwOw">[12]</span></a></sup> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dog_breed" title="Dog breed" id="mwPA">Dog breeds</a> vary widely in shape, size, and color. They perform many roles for humans, such as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hunting_dog" title="Hunting dog" id="mwPQ">hunting</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Herding_dog" title="Herding dog" id="mwPg">herding</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sled_dog" title="Sled dog" id="mwPw">pulling loads</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Guard_dog" title="Guard dog" id="mwQA">protection</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Police_dog" title="Police dog" id="mwQQ">assisting police</a> and the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dogs_in_warfare" title="Dogs in warfare" id="mwQg">military</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pet" title="Pet" id="mwQw">companionship</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Therapy_dog" title="Therapy dog" id="mwRA">therapy</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Service_dog" title="Service dog" class="mw-redirect" id="mwRQ">aiding disabled people</a>. Over the millennia, dogs became uniquely adapted to human behavior, and the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Human-canine_bond" title="Human-canine bond" class="mw-redirect" id="mwRg">human-canine bond</a> has been a topic of frequent study.<sup about="#mwt61" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-berns2012_13-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"berns2012"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-berns2012-13" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 13;" id="mwRw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwSA">[13]</span></a></sup> This influence on human society has given them the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sobriquet" title="Sobriquet" id="mwSQ">sobriquet</a> of "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Man's_best_friend" title="Man's best friend" id="mwSg">man's best friend</a>".<sup about="#mwt62" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Derr_14-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Derr"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Derr-14" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 14;" id="mwSw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwTA">[14]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="1" id="mwTQ"><h2 id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt63" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"further","href":"./Template:Further"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Canis lupus dingo#Taxonomic debate – the domestic dog, dingo, and New Guinea singing dog"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwTg"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt63" id="mwTw">Further information: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Canis_lupus_dingo#Taxonomic_debate_–_the_domestic_dog,_dingo,_and_New_Guinea_singing_dog" title="Canis lupus dingo">Canis lupus dingo §<span typeof="mw:Entity"> </span>Taxonomic debate – the domestic dog, dingo, and New Guinea singing dog</a></div>

<p id="mwUA">In 1758, the Swedish botanist and zoologist <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus" id="mwUQ">Carl Linnaeus</a> published in his <i id="mwUg"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae" id="mwUw">Systema Naturae</a></i>, the two-word naming of species (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature" id="mwVA">binomial nomenclature</a>). <i id="mwVQ"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Canis" title="Canis" id="mwVg">Canis</a></i> is the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Latin" title="Latin" id="mwVw">Latin</a> word meaning "dog",<sup about="#mwt68" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWangTedford200858_15-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Wang"},"2":{"wt":"Tedford"},"3":{"wt":"2008"},"p":{"wt":"58"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWangTedford200858-15" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 15;" id="mwWA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwWQ">[15]</span></a></sup> and under this <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Genus" title="Genus" id="mwWg">genus</a>, he listed the domestic dog, the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wolf" title="Wolf" id="mwWw">wolf</a>, and the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Golden_jackal" title="Golden jackal" id="mwXA">golden jackal</a>. He classified the domestic dog as <i id="mwXQ">Canis familiaris</i> and, on the next page, classified the grey wolf as <i id="mwXg">Canis lupus</i>.<sup about="#mwt71" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-linnaeus1758_2-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"linnaeus1758"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-linnaeus1758-2" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 2;" id="mwXw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwYA">[2]</span></a></sup> Linnaeus considered the dog to be a separate species from the wolf because of its upturning tail (<i id="mwYQ">cauda recurvata</i>), which is not found in any other <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Canid" title="Canid" class="mw-redirect" id="mwYg">canid</a>.<sup about="#mwt72" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Clutton-Brock1995_16-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Clutton-Brock1995"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Clutton-Brock1995-16" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 16;" id="mwYw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwZA">[16]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwZQ">In 1999, a study of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA" id="mwZg">mitochondrial DNA</a> (mtDNA) indicated that the domestic dog may have originated from the grey wolf, with the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dingo" title="Dingo" id="mwZw">dingo</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./New_Guinea_singing_dog" title="New Guinea singing dog" id="mwaA">New Guinea singing dog</a> breeds having developed at a time when human communities were more isolated from each other.<sup about="#mwt77" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-wayne1999_17-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"wayne1999"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-wayne1999-17" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 17;" id="mwaQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwag">[17]</span></a></sup> In the third edition of <i id="mwaw"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mammal_Species_of_the_World" title="Mammal Species of the World" id="mwbA">Mammal Species of the World</a></i> published in 2005, the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mammalogist" title="Mammalogist" class="mw-redirect" id="mwbQ">mammalogist</a> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./W._Christopher_Wozencraft" title="W. Christopher Wozencraft" class="mw-redirect" id="mwbg">W. Christopher Wozencraft</a> listed under the wolf <i id="mwbw">Canis lupus</i> its wild subspecies and proposed two additional subspecies, which formed the domestic dog clade: <i id="mwcA">familiaris</i>, as named by Linnaeus in 1758 and, <i id="mwcQ">dingo</i> named by Meyer in 1793. Wozencraft included <i id="mwcg">hallstromi</i> (the New Guinea singing dog) as another name (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Junior_synonym" title="Junior synonym" class="mw-redirect" id="mwcw">junior synonym</a>) for the dingo. Wozencraft referred to the mtDNA study as one of the guides informing his decision.<sup about="#mwt78" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-wozencraft2005_3-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"wozencraft2005"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-wozencraft2005-3" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 3;" id="mwdA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwdQ">[3]</span></a></sup> Mammalogists have noted the inclusion of <i id="mwdg">familiaris</i> and <i id="mwdw">dingo</i> together under the "domestic dog" clade<sup about="#mwt79" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-jackson2017_18-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"jackson2017"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-jackson2017-18" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 18;" id="mweA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mweQ">[18]</span></a></sup> with some debating it.<sup about="#mwt80" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-smithC1_19-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"smithC1"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-smithC1-19" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 19;" id="mweg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwew">[19]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwfA">In 2019, a workshop hosted by the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./IUCN" title="IUCN" class="mw-redirect" id="mwfQ">IUCN</a>/Species Survival Commission's Canid Specialist Group considered the dingo and the New Guinea singing dog to be <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Feral_dog" title="Feral dog" class="mw-redirect" id="mwfg">feral</a> <i id="mwfw">Canis familiaris</i> and therefore did not assess them for the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List" id="mwgA">IUCN Red List of Threatened Species</a>.<sup about="#mwt82" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Alvares2019_4-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Alvares2019"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Alvares2019-4" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 4;" id="mwgQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwgg">[4]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="2" id="mwgw"><h2 id="Evolution">Evolution</h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt83" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Evolution of the wolf"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwhA"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt83" id="mwhQ">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Evolution_of_the_wolf" title="Evolution of the wolf">Evolution of the wolf</a></div>

<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwhg"><a href="./File:Brechschere-Hund.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwhw"><img resource="./File:Brechschere-Hund.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Brechschere-Hund.jpg/180px-Brechschere-Hund.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="2400" data-file-type="bitmap" height="270" width="180" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Brechschere-Hund.jpg/270px-Brechschere-Hund.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Brechschere-Hund.jpg/360px-Brechschere-Hund.jpg 2x" id="mwiA"/></a><figcaption id="mwiQ">Location of a dog's <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Carnassial" title="Carnassial" id="mwig">carnassials</a>; the inside of the 4th upper <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Premolar" title="Premolar" id="mwiw">premolar</a> aligns with the outside of the 1st lower <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Molar_(tooth)" title="Molar (tooth)" id="mwjA">molar</a>, working like scissor blades</figcaption></figure>

<p id="mwjQ">The <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cretaceous–Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event" id="mwjg">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a> occurred 66 million years ago and brought an end to the non-avian <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur" id="mwjw">dinosaurs</a> and the appearance of the first carnivorans.<sup about="#mwt86" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWangTedford20088_20-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Wang"},"2":{"wt":"Tedford"},"3":{"wt":"2008"},"p":{"wt":"8"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWangTedford20088-20" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 20;" id="mwkA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwkQ">[20]</span></a></sup> The name <i id="mwkg">carnivoran</i> is given to a member of the order <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Carnivora" title="Carnivora" id="mwkw">Carnivora</a>. Carnivorans possess a common arrangement of teeth called <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Carnassials" title="Carnassials" class="mw-redirect" id="mwlA">carnassials</a>, in which the first lower <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Molar_(tooth)" title="Molar (tooth)" id="mwlQ">molar</a> and the last upper <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Premolar" title="Premolar" id="mwlg">premolar</a> possess blade-like enamel <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Crown_(tooth)" title="Crown (tooth)" id="mwlw">crowns</a> that act similar to a pair of shears for cutting meat. This dental arrangement has been modified by adaptation over the past 60 million years for diets composed of meat, for crushing vegetation, or for the loss of the carnassial function altogether as in seals, sea lions, and walruses. Today, not all carnivorans are <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Carnivores" title="Carnivores" class="mw-redirect" id="mwmA">carnivores</a>, such as the insect-eating <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Aardwolf" title="Aardwolf" id="mwmQ">aardwolf</a>.<sup about="#mwt89" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWangTedford20081_5-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Wang"},"2":{"wt":"Tedford"},"3":{"wt":"2008"},"p":{"wt":"1"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWangTedford20081-5" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 5;" id="mwmg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwmw">[5]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwnA">The carnivoran ancestors of the dog-like <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Caniforms" title="Caniforms" class="mw-redirect" id="mwnQ">caniforms</a> and the cat-like <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Feliforms" title="Feliforms" class="mw-redirect" id="mwng">feliforms</a> began their separate evolutionary paths just after the end of the dinosaurs. The first members of the dog family <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Canidae" title="Canidae" id="mwnw">Canidae</a> appeared 40 million years ago,<sup about="#mwt92" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWangTedford2008116_21-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Wang"},"2":{"wt":"Tedford"},"3":{"wt":"2008"},"p":{"wt":"116"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWangTedford2008116-21" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 21;" id="mwoA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwoQ">[21]</span></a></sup> of which only its subfamily the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Caninae" title="Caninae" id="mwog">Caninae</a> survives today in the form of the wolf-like and fox-like canines. Within the Caninae, the first members of genus <i id="mwow"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Canis" title="Canis" id="mwpA">Canis</a></i> appeared six million years ago,<sup about="#mwt95" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWangTedford200858_15-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Wang"},"2":{"wt":"Tedford"},"3":{"wt":"2008"},"p":{"wt":"58"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWangTedford200858-15" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 15;" id="mwpQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwpg">[15]</span></a></sup> the ancestors of modern domestic dogs, wolves, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Coyotes" title="Coyotes" class="mw-redirect" id="mwpw">coyotes</a>, and golden jackals.</p>

<section data-mw-section-id="3" id="mwqA"><h3 id="Domestication">Domestication</h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt98" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Domestication of the dog"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwqQ"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt98" id="mwqg">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Domestication_of_the_dog" title="Domestication of the dog">Domestication of the dog</a></div>

<p id="mwqw">The earliest remains generally accepted to be those of a domesticated dog were discovered in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Oberkassel,_Bonn" title="Oberkassel, Bonn" id="mwrA">Bonn-Oberkassel</a>, Germany. Contextual, isotopic, genetic, and morphological evidence shows that this dog was not a local wolf.<sup about="#mwt105" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Perri2021_22-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Perri2021"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Perri2021-22" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 22;" id="mwrQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwrg">[22]</span></a></sup> The dog was dated to 14,223 years ago and was found buried along with a man and a woman, all three having been sprayed with red <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hematite" title="Hematite" id="mwrw">hematite</a> powder and buried under large, thick basalt blocks. The dog had died of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Canine_distemper" title="Canine distemper" id="mwsA">canine distemper</a>.<sup about="#mwt106" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-janssens2018_23-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"janssens2018"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-janssens2018-23" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 23;" id="mwsQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwsg">[23]</span></a></sup> Earlier remains dating back to 30,000 years ago have been described as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Paleolithic_dog" title="Paleolithic dog" id="mwsw">Paleolithic dogs</a>, but their status as dogs or wolves remains debated<sup about="#mwt107" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Irving-Pease2018_24-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Irving-Pease2018"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Irving-Pease2018-24" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 24;" id="mwtA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwtQ">[24]</span></a></sup> because considerable <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)" id="mwtg">morphological</a> diversity existed among wolves during the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Late_Pleistocene" title="Late Pleistocene" id="mwtw">Late Pleistocene</a>.<sup about="#mwt108" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Thalmann2018_1-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Thalmann2018"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Thalmann2018-1" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 1;" id="mwuA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwuQ">[1]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwug">This timing indicates that the dog was the first species to be domesticated<sup about="#mwt120" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-larson2014_9-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"larson2014"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-larson2014-9" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 9;" id="mwuw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwvA">[9]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt121" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-freedman2017_8-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"freedman2017"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-freedman2017-8" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 8;" id="mwvQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwvg">[8]</span></a></sup> in the time of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hunter–gatherers" title="Hunter–gatherers" class="mw-redirect" id="mwvw">hunter–gatherers</a>,<sup about="#mwt122" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Frantz2020_7-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Frantz2020"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Frantz2020-7" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 7;" id="mwwA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwwQ">[7]</span></a></sup> which predates agriculture.<sup about="#mwt123" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Thalmann2018_1-3" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Thalmann2018"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Thalmann2018-1" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 1;" id="mwwg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwww">[1]</span></a></sup> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./DNA_sequences" title="DNA sequences" class="mw-redirect" id="mwxA">DNA sequences</a> show that all ancient and modern dogs share a common ancestry and descended from an ancient, extinct wolf population which was distinct from the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wolf" title="Wolf" id="mwxQ">modern wolf</a> lineage.<sup about="#mwt124" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Bergström2020_6-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Bergström2020"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Bergström2020-6" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 6;" id="mwxg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwxw">[6]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt125" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Frantz2020_7-3" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Frantz2020"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Frantz2020-7" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 7;" id="mwyA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwyQ">[7]</span></a></sup> Most dogs form a sister group to the remains of a Late <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pleistocene_wolf" title="Pleistocene wolf" id="mwyg">Pleistocene wolf</a> found in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink/Interwiki" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kesslerloch" title="de:Kesslerloch" class="extiw" id="mwyw">Kessleroch cave</a> near <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Thayngen#Heritage_sites_of_national_significance" title="Thayngen" id="mwzA">Thayngen</a> in the canton of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Schaffhausen" title="Schaffhausen" id="mwzQ">Schaffhausen</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Switzerland" title="Switzerland" id="mwzg">Switzerland</a>, which dates to 14,500 years ago. The <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Most_recent_common_ancestor" title="Most recent common ancestor" id="mwzw">most recent common ancestor</a> of both is estimated to be from 32,100 years ago.<sup about="#mwt126" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-thalmann2013_25-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"thalmann2013"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-thalmann2013-25" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 25;" id="mw0A"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw0Q">[25]</span></a></sup> This indicates that an extinct Late Pleistocene wolf may have been the ancestor of the dog,<sup about="#mwt127" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-freedman2017_8-3" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"freedman2017"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-freedman2017-8" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 8;" id="mw0g"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw0w">[8]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt128" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Thalmann2018_1-4" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Thalmann2018"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Thalmann2018-1" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 1;" id="mw1A"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw1Q">[1]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt129" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Lord2020_26-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Lord2020"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Lord2020-26" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 26;" id="mw1g"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw1w">[26]</span></a></sup> with the modern wolf being the dog's nearest living relative.<sup about="#mwt130" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-freedman2017_8-4" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"freedman2017"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-freedman2017-8" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 8;" id="mw2A"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw2Q">[8]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mw2g">The dog is a classic example of a domestic animal that likely travelled a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Commensalism" title="Commensalism" id="mw2w">commensal</a> pathway into domestication.<sup about="#mwt136" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Irving-Pease2018_24-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Irving-Pease2018"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Irving-Pease2018-24" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 24;" id="mw3A"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw3Q">[24]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt137" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-larson2012_27-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"larson2012"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-larson2012-27" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 27;" id="mw3g"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw3w">[27]</span></a></sup> The questions of when and where dogs were first domesticated have taxed geneticists and archaeologists for decades.<sup about="#mwt138" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-larson2014_9-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"larson2014"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-larson2014-9" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 9;" id="mw4A"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw4Q">[9]</span></a></sup> Genetic studies suggest a domestication process commencing over 25,000 years ago, in one or several wolf populations in either Europe, the high Arctic, or eastern Asia.<sup about="#mwt139" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Ostrander2019_10-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Ostrander2019"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Ostrander2019-10" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 10;" id="mw4g"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw4w">[10]</span></a></sup> In 2021, a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Literature_review" title="Literature review" id="mw5A">literature review</a> of the current evidence  <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Inference" title="Inference" id="mw5Q">infers</a> that the dog was domesticated in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Siberia" title="Siberia" id="mw5g">Siberia</a> 23,000 years ago by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ancient_North_Siberians" title="Ancient North Siberians" class="mw-redirect" id="mw5w">ancient North Siberians</a>, then later dispersed eastward into the Americas and westward across Eurasia.<sup about="#mwt140" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Perri2021_22-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Perri2021"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Perri2021-22" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 22;" id="mw6A"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw6Q">[22]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="4" id="mw6g"><h3 id="Breeds">Breeds</h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt141" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Dog breed"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mw6w"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt141" id="mw7A">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dog_breed" title="Dog breed">Dog breed</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt144" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"further","href":"./Template:Further"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Dog type"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mw7Q"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt144" id="mw7g">Further information: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dog_type" title="Dog type">Dog type</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mw7w"><a href="./File:Big_and_little_dog.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mw8A"><img resource="./File:Big_and_little_dog.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Big_and_little_dog.jpg/220px-Big_and_little_dog.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="309" data-file-type="bitmap" height="113" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Big_and_little_dog.jpg/330px-Big_and_little_dog.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Big_and_little_dog.jpg/440px-Big_and_little_dog.jpg 2x" id="mw8Q"/></a><figcaption id="mw8g">Dog breeds show a range of phenotypic variation</figcaption></figure>

<p id="mw8w">Dogs are the most variable mammal on earth with around 450 globally recognized <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dog_breeds" title="Dog breeds" class="mw-redirect" id="mw9A">dog breeds</a>.<sup about="#mwt157" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Ostrander2019_10-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Ostrander2019"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Ostrander2019-10" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 10;" id="mw9Q"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw9g">[10]</span></a></sup> In the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Victorian_era" title="Victorian era" id="mw9w">Victorian era</a>, directed human <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Selection_(biology)" title="Selection (biology)" class="mw-redirect" id="mw-A">selection</a> developed the modern <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dog_breeds" title="Dog breeds" class="mw-redirect" id="mw-Q">dog breeds</a>, which resulted in a vast range of phenotypes.<sup about="#mwt158" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-freedman2017_8-5" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"freedman2017"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-freedman2017-8" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 8;" id="mw-g"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw-w">[8]</span></a></sup> Most breeds were derived from small numbers of founders within the last 200 years,<sup about="#mwt159" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-freedman2017_8-6" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"freedman2017"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-freedman2017-8" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 8;" id="mw_A"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw_Q">[8]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt160" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Ostrander2019_10-3" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Ostrander2019"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Ostrander2019-10" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 10;" id="mw_g"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw_w">[10]</span></a></sup> and since then dogs have undergone rapid <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Phenotypic" title="Phenotypic" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAQA">phenotypic</a> change and were formed into today's modern breeds due to <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Artificial_selection" title="Artificial selection" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAQE">artificial selection</a> imposed by humans. The skull, body, and limb proportions vary significantly between breeds, with dogs displaying more phenotypic diversity than can be found within the entire order of carnivores. These breeds possess distinct traits related to morphology, which include body size, skull shape, tail phenotype, fur type and colour.<sup about="#mwt161" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-freedman2017_8-7" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"freedman2017"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-freedman2017-8" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 8;" id="mwAQI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAQM">[8]</span></a></sup> Their behavioural traits include guarding, herding, and hunting,<sup about="#mwt162" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-freedman2017_8-8" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"freedman2017"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-freedman2017-8" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 8;" id="mwAQQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAQU">[8]</span></a></sup> retrieving, and scent detection. Their personality traits include hypersocial behavior, boldness, and aggression,<sup about="#mwt163" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Ostrander2019_10-4" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Ostrander2019"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Ostrander2019-10" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 10;" id="mwAQY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAQc">[10]</span></a></sup> which demonstrates the functional and behavioral diversity of dogs.<sup about="#mwt164" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-freedman2017_8-9" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"freedman2017"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-freedman2017-8" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 8;" id="mwAQg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAQk">[8]</span></a></sup> As a result, present day dogs are the most abundant carnivore species and are dispersed around the world.<sup about="#mwt165" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Ostrander2019_10-5" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Ostrander2019"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Ostrander2019-10" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 10;" id="mwAQo"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAQs">[10]</span></a></sup> The most striking example of this dispersal is that of the numerous modern breeds of European lineage during the Victorian era.<sup about="#mwt166" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Frantz2020_7-4" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Frantz2020"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Frantz2020-7" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 7;" id="mwAQw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAQ0">[7]</span></a></sup></p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAQ4"><a href="./File:Bangladeshi_Dog.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAQ8"><img resource="./File:Bangladeshi_Dog.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Bangladeshi_Dog.jpg/220px-Bangladeshi_Dog.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="4096" data-file-height="1840" data-file-type="bitmap" height="99" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Bangladeshi_Dog.jpg/330px-Bangladeshi_Dog.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Bangladeshi_Dog.jpg/440px-Bangladeshi_Dog.jpg 2x" id="mwARA"/></a><figcaption id="mwARE">Bangladeshi Dog</figcaption></figure>

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="5" id="mwARI"><h2 id="Biology">Biology</h2>

<section data-mw-section-id="6" id="mwARM"><h3 id="Anatomy">Anatomy</h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt167" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Dog anatomy"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwARQ"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt167" id="mwARU">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dog_anatomy" title="Dog anatomy">Dog anatomy</a></div>

<section data-mw-section-id="7" id="mwARY"><h4 id="Skeleton">Skeleton</h4>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwARc"><a href="./File:Dog_anatomy_lateral_skeleton_view.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwARg"><img resource="./File:Dog_anatomy_lateral_skeleton_view.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Dog_anatomy_lateral_skeleton_view.jpg/280px-Dog_anatomy_lateral_skeleton_view.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1239" data-file-type="bitmap" height="231" width="280" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Dog_anatomy_lateral_skeleton_view.jpg/420px-Dog_anatomy_lateral_skeleton_view.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Dog_anatomy_lateral_skeleton_view.jpg/560px-Dog_anatomy_lateral_skeleton_view.jpg 2x" id="mwARk"/></a><figcaption id="mwARo">A lateral view of a dog skeleton</figcaption></figure>
<p id="mwARs">All healthy dogs, regardless of their size and type, have an identical <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Skeleton" title="Skeleton" id="mwARw">skeletal</a> structure with the exception of the number of bones in the tail, although there is significant skeletal variation between dogs of different types.<sup about="#mwt170" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe200412_28-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfnp","href":"./Template:Sfnp"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Cunliffe"},"2":{"wt":"2004"},"p":{"wt":"12"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe200412-28" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 28;" id="mwAR0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAR4">[28]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt173" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFogle200938–39_29-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfnp","href":"./Template:Sfnp"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Fogle"},"2":{"wt":"2009"},"pp":{"wt":"38-39"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFogle200938–39-29" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 29;" id="mwAR8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwASA">[29]</span></a></sup> The dog's skeleton is well adapted for running; the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Vertebra" title="Vertebra" id="mwASE">vertebrae</a> on the neck and back have extensions for powerful back muscles to connect to, the long ribs provide plenty of room for the heart and lungs, and the shoulders are unattached to the skeleton allowing great flexibility.<sup about="#mwt176" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe200412_28-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfnp","href":"./Template:Sfnp"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Cunliffe"},"2":{"wt":"2004"},"p":{"wt":"12"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe200412-28" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 28;" id="mwASI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwASM">[28]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt179" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFogle200938–39_29-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfnp","href":"./Template:Sfnp"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Fogle"},"2":{"wt":"2009"},"pp":{"wt":"38-39"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFogle200938–39-29" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 29;" id="mwASQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwASU">[29]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwASY">Compared to the dog's wolf-like ancestors, selective breeding since domestication has seen the dog's skeleton greatly enhanced in size for larger types as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mastiff" title="Mastiff" id="mwASc">mastiffs</a> and miniaturised for smaller types such as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Terrier" title="Terrier" id="mwASg">terriers</a>; <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dwarfism" title="Dwarfism" id="mwASk">dwarfism</a> has been selectively utilised for some types where short legs are advantageous such as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dachshund" title="Dachshund" id="mwASo">dachshunds</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Corgi" title="Corgi" class="mw-redirect" id="mwASs">corgis</a>.<sup about="#mwt182" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFogle200938–39_29-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfnp","href":"./Template:Sfnp"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Fogle"},"2":{"wt":"2009"},"pp":{"wt":"38-39"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFogle200938–39-29" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 29;" id="mwASw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAS0">[29]</span></a></sup> Most dogs naturally have 26 vertebrae in their tails, but some with <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Natural_bobtail" title="Natural bobtail" id="mwAS4">naturally short tails</a> have as few as three.<sup about="#mwt185" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe200412_28-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfnp","href":"./Template:Sfnp"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Cunliffe"},"2":{"wt":"2004"},"p":{"wt":"12"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe200412-28" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 28;" id="mwAS8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwATA">[28]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwATE">The dog's skull has identical components regardless of breed type, but there is significant divergence in terms of skull shape between types.<sup about="#mwt188" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFogle200938–39_29-3" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfnp","href":"./Template:Sfnp"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Fogle"},"2":{"wt":"2009"},"pp":{"wt":"38-39"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFogle200938–39-29" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 29;" id="mwATI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwATM">[29]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt191" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJonesHamilton197127_30-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfnp","href":"./Template:Sfnp"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Jones"},"2":{"wt":"Hamilton"},"3":{"wt":"1971"},"p":{"wt":"27"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJonesHamilton197127-30" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 30;" id="mwATQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwATU">[30]</span></a></sup> The three basic skull shapes are the elongated dolichocephalic type as seen in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sighthound" title="Sighthound" id="mwATY">sighthounds</a>, the intermediate mesocephalic or mesaticephalic type, and the very short and broad brachycephalic type exemplified by mastiff type skulls.<sup about="#mwt194" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFogle200938–39_29-4" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfnp","href":"./Template:Sfnp"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Fogle"},"2":{"wt":"2009"},"pp":{"wt":"38-39"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFogle200938–39-29" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 29;" id="mwATc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwATg">[29]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt197" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJonesHamilton197127_30-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfnp","href":"./Template:Sfnp"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Jones"},"2":{"wt":"Hamilton"},"3":{"wt":"1971"},"p":{"wt":"27"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJonesHamilton197127-30" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 30;" id="mwATk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwATo">[30]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="8" id="mwATs"><h4 id="Senses">Senses</h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt200" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"further","href":"./Template:Further"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Dog anatomy#Senses"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwATw"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt200" id="mwAT0">Further information: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dog_anatomy#Senses" title="Dog anatomy">Dog anatomy §<span typeof="mw:Entity"> </span>Senses</a></div>

<p id="mwAT4">A dog's senses include vision, hearing, smell, taste, touch. One study suggested that dogs can feel Earth's magnetic field.<sup about="#mwt207" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-31" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-31"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-31" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 31;" id="mwAT8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAUA">[31]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="9" id="mwAUE"><h4 id="Coat">Coat</h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt208" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Dog coat"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAUI"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt208" id="mwAUM">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dog_coat" title="Dog coat">Dog coat</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAUQ"><a href="./File:Dog_coat_variation.png" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAUU"><img resource="./File:Dog_coat_variation.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Dog_coat_variation.png/220px-Dog_coat_variation.png" decoding="async" data-file-width="768" data-file-height="805" data-file-type="bitmap" height="231" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Dog_coat_variation.png/330px-Dog_coat_variation.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Dog_coat_variation.png/440px-Dog_coat_variation.png 2x" id="mwAUY"/></a><figcaption id="mwAUc">Dogs display wide variation in coat type, density, length, color, and composition</figcaption></figure>

<p id="mwAUg">The <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pelage" title="Pelage" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAUk">coats</a> of domestic dogs are of two varieties: "double" being familiar with dogs (as well as wolves) originating from colder climates, made up of a coarse <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Guard_hair" title="Guard hair" id="mwAUo">guard hair</a> and a soft <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Down_hair" title="Down hair" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAUs">down hair</a>, or "single", with the topcoat only. Breeds may have an occasional "blaze", stripe, or "star" of white fur on their chest or underside.<sup about="#mwt212" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe200422–23_32-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfnp","href":"./Template:Sfnp"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Cunliffe"},"2":{"wt":"2004"},"pp":{"wt":"22–23"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe200422–23-32" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 32;" id="mwAUw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAU0">[32]</span></a></sup> Premature graying can occur in dogs from as early as one year of age; this is associated with <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Impulsivity" title="Impulsivity" id="mwAU4">impulsive behaviors</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Anxiety" title="Anxiety" id="mwAU8">anxiety behaviors</a>, fear of noise, and fear of unfamiliar people or animals.<sup about="#mwt218" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-33" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-33"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-33" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 33;" id="mwAVA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAVE">[33]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="10" id="mwAVI"><h4 id="Tail">Tail</h4>
<p id="mwAVM">There are many different shapes for <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dog_tail" title="Dog tail" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAVQ">dog tails</a>: straight, straight up, sickle, curled, or corkscrew. As with many canids, one of the primary functions of a dog's tail is to communicate their emotional state, which can be crucial in getting along with others. In some hunting dogs the tail is traditionally <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Docking_(dog)" title="Docking (dog)" id="mwAVU">docked</a> to avoid injuries.</p>

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="11" id="mwAVY"><h3 id="Health">Health</h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt219" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Dog health"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAVc"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt219" id="mwAVg">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dog_health" title="Dog health">Dog health</a></div>

<p id="mwAVk">Some breeds of dogs are prone to specific genetic ailments such as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Elbow_dysplasia" title="Elbow dysplasia" id="mwAVo">elbow</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hip_dysplasia_(canine)" title="Hip dysplasia (canine)" id="mwAVs">hip dysplasia</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Blindness" title="Blindness" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAVw">blindness</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Deafness" title="Deafness" id="mwAV0">deafness</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pulmonary_valve_stenosis" title="Pulmonary valve stenosis" id="mwAV4">pulmonic stenosis</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cleft_lip_and_palate" title="Cleft lip and palate" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAV8">cleft palate</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Luxating_patella" title="Luxating patella" id="mwAWA">trick knees</a>. Two severe medical conditions significantly affecting dogs are <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pyometra" title="Pyometra" id="mwAWE">pyometra</a>, affecting <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Neutering" title="Neutering" id="mwAWI">unspayed</a> females of all breeds and ages, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gastric_dilatation_volvulus" title="Gastric dilatation volvulus" id="mwAWM">Gastric dilatation volvulus</a> (bloat), which affects larger breeds or deep-chested dogs. Both of these are acute conditions and can kill rapidly. Dogs are also susceptible to parasites such as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Flea" title="Flea" id="mwAWQ">fleas</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tick" title="Tick" id="mwAWU">ticks</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mite" title="Mite" id="mwAWY">mites</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hookworm" title="Hookworm" id="mwAWc">hookworms</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tapeworm" title="Tapeworm" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAWg">tapeworms</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Roundworm" title="Roundworm" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAWk">roundworms</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dirofilaria_immitis" title="Dirofilaria immitis" id="mwAWo">heartworms</a>, which is a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Roundworm" title="Roundworm" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAWs">roundworm</a> species that lives in the hearts of dogs.</p>

<p id="mwAWw">Several human foods and household ingestible are toxic to dogs, including <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chocolate" title="Chocolate" id="mwAW0">chocolate solids</a>, causing <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Theobromine_poisoning" title="Theobromine poisoning" id="mwAW4">theobromine poisoning</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Onion" title="Onion" id="mwAW8">onions</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Garlic" title="Garlic" id="mwAXA">garlic</a>, causing <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Thiosulphate" title="Thiosulphate" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAXE">thiosulphate</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sulfoxide" title="Sulfoxide" id="mwAXI">sulfoxide</a> or <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Disulfide" title="Disulfide" id="mwAXM">disulfide</a> poisoning, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Grape" title="Grape" id="mwAXQ">grapes</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Raisin" title="Raisin" id="mwAXU">raisins</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Macadamia_nut" title="Macadamia nut" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAXY">macadamia nuts</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Xylitol" title="Xylitol" id="mwAXc">xylitol</a>.<sup about="#mwt228" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-34" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-34"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-34" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 34;" id="mwAXg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAXk">[34]</span></a></sup> The nicotine in tobacco can also be dangerous to dogs. Signs of ingestion can include copious vomiting (e.g., from eating cigar butts) or <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea" id="mwAXo">diarrhea</a>. Some other symptoms are abdominal pain, loss of coordination, collapse, or death.<sup about="#mwt232" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-35" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-35"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-35" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 35;" id="mwAXs"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAXw">[35]</span></a></sup><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_May_2021" about="#mwt224" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"page needed","href":"./Template:Page_needed"},"params":{"date":{"wt":"May 2021"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAX0"/><sup class="noprint Inline-Template " style="white-space:nowrap;" about="#mwt224" id="mwAX4"><span typeof="mw:Entity">[</span><i><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2021)">page<span typeof="mw:Entity"> </span>needed</span></a></i><span typeof="mw:Entity">]</span></sup></p>

<p id="mwAX8">Dogs are also vulnerable to some of the same health conditions as humans, including <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Diabetes_in_dogs" title="Diabetes in dogs" id="mwAYA">diabetes</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tooth_pathology" title="Tooth pathology" id="mwAYE">dental</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease" id="mwAYI">heart disease</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy" id="mwAYM">epilepsy</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cancer" title="Cancer" id="mwAYQ">cancer</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hypothyroidism" title="Hypothyroidism" id="mwAYU">hypothyroidism</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Arthritis" title="Arthritis" id="mwAYY">arthritis</a>.</p>

<section data-mw-section-id="12" id="mwAYc"><h4 id="Lifespan">Lifespan</h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt233" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Further","href":"./Template:Further"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Aging in dogs"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAYg"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt233" id="mwAYk">Further information: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Aging_in_dogs" title="Aging in dogs">Aging in dogs</a></div>

<p id="mwAYo">The typical lifespan of dogs varies widely among breeds, but for most, the median longevity (the age at which half the dogs in a population have died and half are still alive) ranges from 10 to 13 years.<sup about="#mwt245" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Denmark_Survey_36-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Denmark Survey"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Denmark_Survey-36"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Denmark_Survey-36" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 36;" id="mwAYs"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAYw">[36]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt249" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-British_Owners_37-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"British Owners"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-British_Owners-37"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-British_Owners-37" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 37;" id="mwAY0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAY4">[37]</span></a></sup> The median longevity of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mixed-breed_dog" title="Mixed-breed dog" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAY8">mixed-breed dogs</a>, taken as an average of all sizes, is one or more years longer than that of purebred dogs when all breeds are averaged.<sup about="#mwt250" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Denmark_Survey_36-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Denmark Survey"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Denmark_Survey-36" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 36;" id="mwAZA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAZE">[36]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt251" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-British_Owners_37-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"British Owners"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-British_Owners-37" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 37;" id="mwAZI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAZM">[37]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt255" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Vet_School_Data_38-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Vet School Data"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Vet_School_Data-38"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Vet_School_Data-38" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 38;" id="mwAZQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAZU">[38]</span></a></sup> For dogs in England, increased body weight has been found to be negatively correlated with longevity (i.e., the heavier the dog, the shorter its lifespan), and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mixed-breed_dog" title="Mixed-breed dog" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAZY">mixed-breed dogs</a> live on average 1.2 years longer than <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Purebred_dog" title="Purebred dog" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAZc">purebred dogs</a>.<sup about="#mwt256" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-O'Neill2013_39-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"O&apos;Neill2013"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-O'Neill2013-39" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 39;" id="mwAZg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAZk">[39]</span></a></sup></p>

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="13" id="mwAZo"><h3 id="Reproduction">Reproduction</h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt257" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Canine reproduction"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAZs"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt257" id="mwAZw">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Canine_reproduction" title="Canine reproduction">Canine reproduction</a></div>

<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAZ0"><a href="./File:Säugende_Hündin.JPG" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAZ4"><img resource="./File:Säugende_Hündin.JPG" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/S%C3%A4ugende_H%C3%BCndin.JPG/220px-S%C3%A4ugende_H%C3%BCndin.JPG" decoding="async" data-file-width="1108" data-file-height="805" data-file-type="bitmap" height="160" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/S%C3%A4ugende_H%C3%BCndin.JPG/330px-S%C3%A4ugende_H%C3%BCndin.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/S%C3%A4ugende_H%C3%BCndin.JPG/440px-S%C3%A4ugende_H%C3%BCndin.JPG 2x" id="mwAZ8"/></a><figcaption id="mwAaA">A female dog nursing newborn puppies.</figcaption></figure>

<p id="mwAaE">In domestic dogs, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sexual_maturity" title="Sexual maturity" id="mwAaI">sexual maturity</a> happens around six months to one year for both males and females, although this can be delayed until up to two years of age for some large breeds, and is the time at which female dogs will have their first <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Estrous_cycle" title="Estrous cycle" id="mwAaM">estrous cycle</a>. They will experience subsequent estrous cycles semiannually, during which the body prepares for <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy" id="mwAaQ">pregnancy</a>. At the peak of the cycle, females will become estrous, mentally and physically receptive to <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Canine_tying" title="Canine tying" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAaU">copulation</a>. Because the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ovum" title="Ovum" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAaY">ova</a> survive and can be fertilized for a week after ovulation, more than one male can sire the same litter.<sup about="#mwt261" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-ADW_12-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"ADW"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-ADW-12" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 12;" id="mwAac"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAag">[12]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAak">Fertilization typically occurs two to five days after ovulation; 14–16 days after ovulation, the embryo attaches to the uterus and after seven to eight more days, a heartbeat is detectable.<sup about="#mwt267" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-40" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-40"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-40" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 40;" id="mwAao"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAas">[40]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt271" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-41" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-41"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-41" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 41;" id="mwAaw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAa0">[41]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAa4">Dogs bear their litters roughly 58 to 68 days after <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Fertilization" title="Fertilization" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAa8">fertilization</a>,<sup about="#mwt275" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-ADW_12-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"ADW"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-ADW-12" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 12;" id="mwAbA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAbE">[12]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt279" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-42" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-42"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-42" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 42;" id="mwAbI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAbM">[42]</span></a></sup> with an average of 63 days, although the length of gestation can vary. An average litter consists of about six <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Puppy" title="Puppy" id="mwAbQ">puppies</a>.<sup about="#mwt283" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-43" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-43"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-43" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 43;" id="mwAbU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAbY">[43]</span></a></sup></p>

<section data-mw-section-id="14" id="mwAbc"><h4 id="Neutering">Neutering</h4>
<p id="mwAbg"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Neutering" title="Neutering" id="mwAbk">Neutering</a> is the sterilization of animals, usually by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Castration" title="Castration" id="mwAbo">removing the male's testicles</a> or the female's <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ovaries" title="Ovaries" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAbs">ovaries</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Uterus" title="Uterus" id="mwAbw">uterus</a>, to eliminate the ability to procreate and reduce <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sex_drive" title="Sex drive" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAb0">sex drive</a>. Because of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dogs'_overpopulation" title="Dogs' overpopulation" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAb4">dogs' overpopulation</a> in some countries, many animal control agencies, such as the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./American_Society_for_the_Prevention_of_Cruelty_to_Animals" title="American Society for the Prevention of Cruelty to Animals" id="mwAb8">American Society for the Prevention of Cruelty to Animals</a> (ASPCA), advise that dogs not intended for further breeding should be neutered, so that they do not have undesired puppies that may later be euthanized.<sup about="#mwt288" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-44" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-44"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-44" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 44;" id="mwAcA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAcE">[44]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAcI">According to the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Humane_Society_of_the_United_States" title="Humane Society of the United States" id="mwAcM">Humane Society of the United States</a>, three to four million dogs and cats are <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Animal_euthanasia" title="Animal euthanasia" id="mwAcQ">euthanized</a> each year.<sup about="#mwt294" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-45" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-45"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-45" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 45;" id="mwAcU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAcY">[45]</span></a></sup> Many more are confined to cages in shelters because there are many more animals than there are homes. Spaying or castrating dogs helps keep overpopulation down.<sup about="#mwt298" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-46" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-46"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-46" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 46;" id="mwAcc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAcg">[46]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAck">Neutering reduces problems caused by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hypersexuality" title="Hypersexuality" id="mwAco">hypersexuality</a>, especially in male dogs.<sup about="#mwt308" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-47" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-47"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-47" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 47;" id="mwAcs"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAcw">[47]</span></a></sup> Spayed female dogs are less likely to develop cancers affecting the mammary glands, ovaries, and other reproductive organs.<sup about="#mwt312" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-48" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-48"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-48" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 48;" id="mwAc0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAc4">[48]</span></a></sup><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_May_2021" about="#mwt304" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"page needed","href":"./Template:Page_needed"},"params":{"date":{"wt":"May 2021"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAc8"/><sup class="noprint Inline-Template " style="white-space:nowrap;" about="#mwt304" id="mwAdA"><span typeof="mw:Entity">[</span><i><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2021)">page<span typeof="mw:Entity"> </span>needed</span></a></i><span typeof="mw:Entity">]</span></sup> However, neutering increases the risk of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Urinary_incontinence" title="Urinary incontinence" id="mwAdE">urinary incontinence</a> in female dogs<sup about="#mwt316" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-49" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-49"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-49" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 49;" id="mwAdI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAdM">[49]</span></a></sup> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Prostate_cancer" title="Prostate cancer" id="mwAdQ">prostate cancer</a> in males<sup about="#mwt320" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-50" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-50"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-50" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 50;" id="mwAdU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAdY">[50]</span></a></sup> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Osteosarcoma" title="Osteosarcoma" id="mwAdc">osteosarcoma</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hemangiosarcoma" title="Hemangiosarcoma" id="mwAdg">hemangiosarcoma</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cruciate_ligament" title="Cruciate ligament" id="mwAdk">cruciate ligament</a> rupture, obesity, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Diabetes_mellitus" title="Diabetes mellitus" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAdo">diabetes mellitus</a> in either sex.<sup about="#mwt324" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-51" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-51"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-51" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 51;" id="mwAds"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAdw">[51]</span></a></sup></p>

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="15" id="mwAd0"><h3 id="Inbreeding_depression">Inbreeding depression</h3>
<p id="mwAd4">A common breeding practice for pet dogs is mating between close relatives (e.g., between half and full siblings).<sup about="#mwt331" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Leroy_52-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Leroy"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Leroy-52"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Leroy-52" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 52;" id="mwAd8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAeA">[52]</span></a></sup> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Inbreeding_depression" title="Inbreeding depression" id="mwAeE">Inbreeding depression</a> is considered to be due mainly to the expression of homozygous deleterious recessive mutations.<sup about="#mwt335" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19834483_53-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"pmid19834483"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-pmid19834483-53"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-pmid19834483-53" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 53;" id="mwAeI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAeM">[53]</span></a></sup> Outcrossing between unrelated individuals, including dogs of different breeds, results in the beneficial masking of deleterious recessive mutations in progeny.<sup about="#mwt339" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-pmid3324702_54-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"pmid3324702"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-pmid3324702-54"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-pmid3324702-54" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 54;" id="mwAeQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAeU">[54]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAeY">In a study of seven dog breeds (the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bernese_Mountain_Dog" title="Bernese Mountain Dog" id="mwAec">Bernese Mountain Dog</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Basset_Hound" title="Basset Hound" id="mwAeg">Basset Hound</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cairn_Terrier" title="Cairn Terrier" id="mwAek">Cairn Terrier</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Brittany_(dog)" title="Brittany (dog)" id="mwAeo">Brittany</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./German_Shepherd" title="German Shepherd" id="mwAes">German Shepherd Dog</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Leonberger" title="Leonberger" id="mwAew">Leonberger</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./West_Highland_White_Terrier" title="West Highland White Terrier" id="mwAe0">West Highland White Terrier</a>), it was found that inbreeding decreases litter size and survival.<sup about="#mwt346" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-pmid25475165_55-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"pmid25475165"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-pmid25475165-55"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-pmid25475165-55" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 55;" id="mwAe4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAe8">[55]</span></a></sup> Another analysis of data on 42,855 <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dachshund" title="Dachshund" id="mwAfA">Dachshund</a> litters found that as the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Inbreeding_coefficient" title="Inbreeding coefficient" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAfE">inbreeding coefficient</a> increased, litter size decreased and the percentage of stillborn puppies increased, thus indicating inbreeding depression.<sup about="#mwt350" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15803761_56-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"pmid15803761"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-pmid15803761-56"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-pmid15803761-56" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 56;" id="mwAfI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAfM">[56]</span></a></sup> In a study of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Boxer_(dog)" title="Boxer (dog)" id="mwAfQ">Boxer</a> litters, 22% of puppies died before reaching 7 weeks of age. Stillbirth was the most frequent cause of death, followed by infection. Mortality due to infection increased significantly with increases in inbreeding.<sup about="#mwt354" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Beek_57-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Beek"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Beek-57"}}'><a href="./Dog#cite_note-Beek-57" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 57;" id="mwAfU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAfY">[57]</span></a></sup></p>

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="16" id="mwAfc"><h2 id="Behavior">Behavior</h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt355" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Dog behavior"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAfg"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt355" id="mwAfk">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dog_behavior" title="Dog behavior">Dog behavior</a></div>
